  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_4433184d-5aa7-4e9b-a1d6-ad0bd1b489c9'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer7522526463708673524">Tristram Foxe and his wife, Beatrice-Joanna, live in their skyscraper world where official family limitation glorifies homosexuality. Eventually, their world is transformed into a chaos of cannibalistic dining-clubs, fantastic fertility rituals, and wars without anger. It is a novel both extravagantly funny and grimly serious.</span>

<div id="review_330892079" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/330892079" />
    <a title="Tom Lucas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9296878-tom-lucas"><img alt="Tom Lucas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1336256000p2/9296878.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/330892079?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom Lucas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom Lucas" href="/user/show/9296878-tom-lucas">Tom Lucas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer330892079" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6004533521798343046">For the most part I like people, even though many of them suck. I am also convinced that the world grows a bit more stupid every day and that we slowly move away from any kind of social evolution. Sure, there's plenty of technological innovation, and dentistry is a far better experience today, but people don't seem to be improving.<br/><br/>We still love screwing each other over, arguing about false issues, and murdering each other. Infrastructures are straining under corruption, graft, and greed. Congres</span>
  <span id="freeText6004533521798343046" style="display:none">For the most part I like people, even though many of them suck. I am also convinced that the world grows a bit more stupid every day and that we slowly move away from any kind of social evolution. Sure, there's plenty of technological innovation, and dentistry is a far better experience today, but people don't seem to be improving.<br /><br />We still love screwing each other over, arguing about false issues, and murdering each other. Infrastructures are straining under corruption, graft, and greed. Congress is highly polarized and our "representatives" do little beyond hooking their friends up and padding the checking account.<br /><br />The worst part of it all is that stupid people just keep breeding.<br /><br />Anthony Burgess, perhaps best known for <i>A Clockwork Orange</i> (most likely you've seen the Kubrick film) had this book published in the same year (1962), and it fits nicely along other literary dystopic works such as <i>1984, Brave New World, and Anthem</i>. However, as much as I loved it, it's probably not in the same weight class.<br /><i>The Wanting Seed</i> begins in a world that is vastly overpopulated, and extreme measures have been institutionalized to handle it. People live in tiny box apartments, homosexuality is the social norm (and it's policed), and everyone eats a protein mush as there just aren't enough damn cows in the world to handle the load. As you wrap your head around this world (seems like it would be easier to just castrate people instead of implemented totalitarian fabulousness), Burgess throws a curve ball and suddenly society collapses.<br /><br />Yep, you're just reading along, dum dum dum dum dum, and hey, the world's ending.<br /><br />The citizens of the world respond to their overcrowding and repression by engaging in mass cannibalism, groovy sex parties, and general mayhem. No, this isn't a spoiler alert, it's on the damn back of the book, so no comments please. Then, as you would imagine, things level out a bit.<br /><br />There's this brainy back story to the book, that Burgess is essentially commenting on the cyclical nature of human history (which you'll also find in <i>A Clockwork Orange</i> and I'm sure in his other books as well). In short, people suck, they have always sucked, and they will continue to suck. So, why not read a good book and forget about it for a while?<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6004533521798343046" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_6134054" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6134054" />
    <a title="Ian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64689-ian"><img alt="Ian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1177015335p2/64689.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6134054?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian" href="/user/show/64689-ian">Ian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of 1984, anyone scared of overpopulation, those who distrust the government.</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6134054" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10598550055331312299">Anthony Burgess is probably best known (at least among Americans) for his novel A Clockwork Orange. Like A Clockwork Orange, The Wanting Seed takes place in a near future society that is in the first stages of decline. <br/><br/>This book is hilarious and contains such things as reprocessing dead humans, promoting homosexual behavior as a way of population control, famine, and fake wars for the benefit of humanity as a whole. These things may not sound funny, but Burgess's language skills shine here, and </span>
  <span id="freeText10598550055331312299" style="display:none">Anthony Burgess is probably best known (at least among Americans) for his novel A Clockwork Orange. Like A Clockwork Orange, The Wanting Seed takes place in a near future society that is in the first stages of decline. <br /><br />This book is hilarious and contains such things as reprocessing dead humans, promoting homosexual behavior as a way of population control, famine, and fake wars for the benefit of humanity as a whole. These things may not sound funny, but Burgess's language skills shine here, and offer great commentary on the danger of becoming obsessed with our survival while we forget why we want to survive in the first place. There are many twists and turns in the book, and it should be read for the ending alone. I'll give a hint: it has something to do with perpetual war.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10598550055331312299" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer526428893" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16262252367629561133">If you enjoy lackluster writing, prejudices from 30 years ago, unrelatable characters, and inexplicable plot twists, then this is the novel for you! If these things annoy you as much as they annoy me, then this is probably not worth reading.<br/><br/>Honestly, the most amusing part of the novel was completely unintentional, because things we take for granted in modern society (Biracial people! Gays! Non-conformity to gender norms! VEGETARIANS!) are the crux of what makes this future world a dystopia. It r</span>
  <span id="freeText16262252367629561133" style="display:none">If you enjoy lackluster writing, prejudices from 30 years ago, unrelatable characters, and inexplicable plot twists, then this is the novel for you! If these things annoy you as much as they annoy me, then this is probably not worth reading.<br /><br />Honestly, the most amusing part of the novel was completely unintentional, because things we take for granted in modern society (Biracial people! Gays! Non-conformity to gender norms! VEGETARIANS!) are the crux of what makes this future world a dystopia. It reminded me of a late-written Agatha Christie novel, where the writer's bitterness and disapproval of the way society is swinging is palpable. Except instead of having interesting plot keeping you involved, all you have in The Wanting Seed is Tristam and Beatrice-Joanna, wandering around being dull and confusing (Now he hates her! Now he's crazy! Now she's giving birth in an outhouse! NOW HE LOVES HER AND FORGIVES HER!) I finished this book mostly because Burgess' terror at what is completely normal now was mildly amusing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16262252367629561133" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15202184507220052748">I love this book. I haven't read Clockwork Orange, but I have read many of Anthony Burgess' other books and this is by far my favorite. The story is set in an extremely overpopulated future. Fascinating plot, intriguing ideas, plenty of social commentary and Burgess' signature use of made up words. Starts a little slow (as most of his books do) but once you get into it you won't be able to put it down. Definitely leaves you thinking when you finish it. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1491960026" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1491960026" />
    <a title="Ben" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22696697-ben"><img alt="Ben" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586816425p2/22696697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1491960026?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben" href="/user/show/22696697-ben">Ben</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22696697-ben?shelf=dystopian-post-apocalyptic">dystopian-post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22696697-ben?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1491960026" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11939160964160827331">What a peculiar novel - Anthony Burgess certainly has a marvellously wicked mind to come up with premises such as this and that in A Clockwork Orange. It tells the story of how society crumbles around the main characters, Tristam Foxe and Beatrice-Joanna, as overpopulation drives it into anarchical behaviour, cannibalism and chaotic orgies in revolt of the Malthusian world and the government's strict anti-natal policies. <br/>I originally wanted to read this book after seeing it referenced in a revie</span>
  <span id="freeText11939160964160827331" style="display:none">What a peculiar novel - Anthony Burgess certainly has a marvellously wicked mind to come up with premises such as this and that in A Clockwork Orange. It tells the story of how society crumbles around the main characters, Tristam Foxe and Beatrice-Joanna, as overpopulation drives it into anarchical behaviour, cannibalism and chaotic orgies in revolt of the Malthusian world and the government's strict anti-natal policies. <br />I originally wanted to read this book after seeing it referenced in a review paper on Calhoun's rodents, which investigated the effect of overpopulation on the psychology and behaviour of rats in a utopian environment. The idea behind it interested me greatly and I was eager to see Burgess' interpretation.<br />What I didn't expect was the idea of this 'dystopia' being a rather attractive society to live in - one where homosexuality is not only legal but promoted and religion is absent. The entire narrative is laced with repugnant prejudices, which was to be expected from a novel written in the 1960s - however it was laughable to me that something which seemed to be viewed as a terrifying future in this book is rightfully accepted today. I eventually learned to just grit my teeth and bear it, although it originally made it hard to sympathise with any of the characters. Beware those who are easily offended, there is some especially incendiary stuff in here.<br />Burgess' vocabulary is astonishing, and his writing style is engrossing - I learned many words which I have never heard or seen before, words which I struggled to even find definitions for in standard dictionaries. However, this did get a bit difficult at times, and his constant referral to the 'cycle of society', from Pelphase to interphase to Gusphase, was slightly confusing. Despite this, the writing was wondrous and a pleasure to read.<br />What I do see missing is how this book was comedic - I didn't find myself laughing all that much, contrary to the quote on the cover which says it is "fantastically funny". Maybe I just don't connect with that level of humour. <br />Overall, a pretty decent read. If you can get past the abominable homophobia and other offensive language and ideologies and concentrate on the intriguing and thrilling storyline, it would be well worth it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11939160964160827331" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Stephanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/815646-stephanie"><img alt="Stephanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1202936663p2/815646.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/30047684?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie" href="/user/show/815646-stephanie">Stephanie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30047684" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3702278162024209590">I am a sucker for a good 1984-esque book. Also, I adore Burgess and all his linguistic talents. I loved his idea of cyclical history, one that is at least somewhat comforting in the midst of current economic crises. This is a must-read for any Burgess fan.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3468672523" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3468672523" />
    <a title="aPriL does feral sometimes " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes"><img alt="aPriL does feral sometimes " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478243665p2/3409532.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3468672523?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="aPriL does feral sometimes " class="user" itemprop="url" name="aPriL does feral sometimes " href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes">aPriL does feral sometimes </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 3450256431" href="/book/show/16153007-the-wanting-seed">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=satire">satire</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=cringed-and-winced">cringed-and-winced</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=pseudo-literary">pseudo-literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=why-does-everyone-love-this-ick-ick">why-does-everyone-love-this-ick-ick</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=finished-reading-but-i-hated-it">finished-reading-but-i-hated-it</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3468672523" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14420150535207040965">‘The Wanting Seed’ by Anthony Burgess is a disguised religious novel by an intellectual who may or may not still have been a Bible believer, but he most certainly retained gender prejudices and a blinkered social paradigm only someone raised in the Catholic Church would have. I think Burgess’s mind was trapped inside a Christian Catholic-themed box with a set number of rigid philosophies. Reading his novels is like living in a world with only two choices possible for every question of self, civi</span>
  <span id="freeText14420150535207040965" style="display:none">‘The Wanting Seed’ by Anthony Burgess is a disguised religious novel by an intellectual who may or may not still have been a Bible believer, but he most certainly retained gender prejudices and a blinkered social paradigm only someone raised in the Catholic Church would have. I think Burgess’s mind was trapped inside a Christian Catholic-themed box with a set number of rigid philosophies. Reading his novels is like living in a world with only two choices possible for every question of self, civilization, politics and social governance. An either-or paradigm of Humanity. Plus, he seemingly enjoyed poking political activists of all sorts into a rage.<br /><br />I should note here when I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41817486.A_Clockwork_Orange" title="A Clockwork Orange by Anthony Burgess" rel="nofollow noopener">A Clockwork Orange</a>, Burgess’s most famous novel, I researched the author. He gave interviews and declared himself, in my opinion, an intellectual Catholic (perhaps a non-believer, or maybe only a philosophical Catholic believer, I don’t know). He felt people needed to be able to be free to choose Good or Evil without government or social interference. I thought his beliefs very childish and incoherent, frankly.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2012/06/04/the-clockwork-condition">https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/20...</a><br /><br />For those of you who have seen the American movie based on the American version of the novel ‘A Clockwork Orange’, the original English novel had a chapter included called the ‘Redemption Chapter’, which was censored in the American version. The book can today be purchased in its fully restored condition in America.<br /><br />‘The Wanting Seed’ supposedly is a satire of current (1962) trends: overpopulation, homosexual rights, growing legal authoritarianism enforcing primarily political leftist thought. Although the book can be understood as a pox on all political houses, I don’t think so. To me, there is an extremist paradigm to the plot carried over into black-and-white religious nonsense, which is typical of satires, but at the same time, I noticed a ‘slight’ attitude of homophobia and anti-government libertarianism.<br /><br /><br />Quotes from ‘The Wanting Seed’:<br /><br /><i>“”There we are, then,”” said Dr. Acheson heartily, a fat gelding of an Anglo-Saxon.”<br /><br />“Beatrice-Joanna looked with distaste, entering the lift, on the embracing giggling pair. The two women, both Caucasian types, were classically complementary—fluffy kitten answered stocky bullfrog. At the fifteenth floor the lift picked up a foppish steatopygous young man, stylish in well-cut jacket without lapels, tight calf-length trousers, flowery round- necked shirt. He turned sharp eyes of distaste on the two lovers, moving his shoulders pettishly, opting with equal disgust at the full womanly presence of Beatrice-Joanna. He began, with swift expert strokes, to make up his face, simpering, as his lips kissed the lipstick, at his reflection in the lift-mirror.”<br /><br />“”It’s the old story. Liberalism prevails, and liberalism means laxness. We leave it to education and propaganda and free contraceptives, abortion clinics and condolences. We encourage non-productive forms of sexual activity. We like to kid ourselves that people are good enough and wise enough to be aware of their responsibilities. But what happens? There was the case, only a few weeks ago, of a couple in Western Province who’d had six children. Six. I ask you. And all alive, too. A very old-fashioned couple—God-followers....””<br /><br />““Well, it’s an old book [the Bible] full of smut. The big sin is to waste your seed, and if God loves you He fills your house with kids...””<br /><br />““We’ve been praying, you know,”said Shonny, pouring out more wine for the ladies, “though, of course, that’s illegal, too. They used to leave us alone in the old days, but now they’ve got these infernal police on the job, spying and arresting, just like in the ancient penal days of sacred memory. We’ve had mass here a couple of times. Father Shackel, God bless and help the poor man, was picked up in his own shop the other day by some of these simperers with guns and lipstick...”</i><br /><br />From beginning to end, the novel is a bitter tirade more than it is a satire. I lost patience. It was more like a libertarian tract which rephrased a single joke for over 200 pages, using homosexual prejudice as the punchline in all of the rephrased variations of the joke. I only recommend the novel to those who have enough of an intellectual streak to explore the ideas without succumbing to its single-dimensional Flatland (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/433567.Flatland_A_Romance_of_Many_Dimensions" title="Flatland A Romance of Many Dimensions by Edwin A. Abbott" rel="nofollow noopener">Flatland: A Romance of Many Dimensions</a>) representation of social values.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14420150535207040965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_254343115" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/254343115" />
    <a title="Eraserhead" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3004469-eraserhead"><img alt="Eraserhead" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1279764454p2/3004469.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/254343115?book_show_action=true">Jan 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eraserhead" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eraserhead" href="/user/show/3004469-eraserhead">Eraserhead</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer254343115" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16942453023675371492">Loved this book. Hilarious and energetic. Comes at you like a psychedelic rock song. I found the story pretty clever but really loved Burgess' sense of apocalypse, as if he transcribed images from Bosch's Garden of Earthly Delights. I loved Tristram's trek thru degenerating England, the slow sprawl of history, moving from extreme police states on opposite poles of the structure.<br/><br/><br/>Yes, the love story was clumsy, but t served the purpose of showing this world's dichotomy and hypocrisy, his wife leav</span>
  <span id="freeText16942453023675371492" style="display:none">Loved this book. Hilarious and energetic. Comes at you like a psychedelic rock song. I found the story pretty clever but really loved Burgess' sense of apocalypse, as if he transcribed images from Bosch's Garden of Earthly Delights. I loved Tristram's trek thru degenerating England, the slow sprawl of history, moving from extreme police states on opposite poles of the structure.<br /><br /><br />Yes, the love story was clumsy, but t served the purpose of showing this world's dichotomy and hypocrisy, his wife leaving him for a fake gay man, being cursed in either worlds for asking questions and being against the establishment no matter who is in charge. The final payoff of the book wasn't as great as the sum of its parts.<br />|<br /><br />An amazing hallucinatory romp through what could be called social commentary, the main purpose of The Wanting Seed is to examine the ebb and flow of political life, how the world seems to veer from one set of rules, of justifications, to the next. The plot is nothing special, but Burgess creates such a skewed and hilarious world of cannibalistic spiritualism, forced sexuality, contrived war that the novel's other flaws are only minor stumbling blocks. He achieves this satire by having his world try to re-create the doings of the past---most hilariously by staging wars to create jobs and keep the population in check.<br /><br />But where the novel really succeeds is representing how each authoritarian figure in the novel grasps almost mindlessly at the next perfect doctrine for controlling the world, be it a general whose only understanding of war is through old movies and the War Poets (a man of many famous first lines) or Tristram's brother, who callously jumps onto each new moral ideal, going from a leader in the INFERTILITY POLICE, needing to hide his illegitimate children in fear of being arrested, to a higher-up in the FERTILITY POLICE, now using those same bastard children as a method of advancing his career.<br /><br /><br />A wondrous, but flawed, novel. You truly get a sense of this sprawling world, and the journey chapters are very effective. <br /><br />Think A Modest Proposal on bad Acid. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16942453023675371492" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1389910643" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1389910643" />
    <a title="Papaphilly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30045612-papaphilly"><img alt="Papaphilly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403407931p2/30045612.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1389910643?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Papaphilly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Papaphilly" href="/user/show/30045612-papaphilly">Papaphilly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30045612-papaphilly?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30045612-papaphilly?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30045612-papaphilly?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1389910643" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14669179844379895010"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8809.The_Wanting_Seed' title='The Wanting Seed by Anthony Burgess' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Wanting Seed</a> is a great read. Part societal study and certainly a criticism of British society. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5735.Anthony_Burgess' title='Anthony Burgess' rel='nofollow noopener'>Anthony Burgess</a> ask what happens to British society if the population overwhelms food supplies. This has been called a comedy, but I am not sure I agree. It is certainly satire, but not so sure it is funny. He certainly lampoons the upper crust and social climbers along with British stoicism, yet it is wrapped in tragedy. <br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8809.The_Wanting_Seed' title='The Wanting Seed by Anthony Burgess' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Wanting Seed</a> watches the death of government, but not of a people and ho</span>
  <span id="freeText14669179844379895010" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8809.The_Wanting_Seed" title="The Wanting Seed by Anthony Burgess" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wanting Seed</a> is a great read. Part societal study and certainly a criticism of British society. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5735.Anthony_Burgess" title="Anthony Burgess" rel="nofollow noopener">Anthony Burgess</a> ask what happens to British society if the population overwhelms food supplies. This has been called a comedy, but I am not sure I agree. It is certainly satire, but not so sure it is funny. He certainly lampoons the upper crust and social climbers along with British stoicism, yet it is wrapped in tragedy. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8809.The_Wanting_Seed" title="The Wanting Seed by Anthony Burgess" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wanting Seed</a> watches the death of government, but not of a people and how somehow they manage to carry on in their own way. How different levels of society first react and then respond to the failing food and their way of rationalization to differentiate themselves from the "others" and their lower ways. It is also a analogy of changing times England faced when the book was written and should be seen in this light. It is a statement of the ordinary average man in changing times where he no longer recognizes his place. Nothing is normal and the world he knows is gone upside down.<br /><br />This is a great read and I have not done the book justice with this review. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8809.The_Wanting_Seed" title="The Wanting Seed by Anthony Burgess" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wanting Seed</a> is well worth the time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14669179844379895010" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_544589381" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/544589381" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/426277-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584954318p2/426277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/544589381?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/426277-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer544589381" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16392823078160519527">Last month I reread Anthony Burgess's most famous novel, <em>A Clockwork Orange</em>. In it I found new insights into Burgess's creative thought, encouraging me to read more of his oeuvre. I followed up on that idea with <em>The Wanting Seed</em>, which he wrote immediately following Clockwork. This dystopian novel demonstrates one of his persistent themes, the conflict between 'Augustinian' authoritarianism and 'neo-Pelagian' liberalism. The novel is set in a future similar to <em>A Clockwork Orange</em>, where Burgess p</span>
  <span id="freeText16392823078160519527" style="display:none">Last month I reread Anthony Burgess's most famous novel, <em>A Clockwork Orange</em>. In it I found new insights into Burgess's creative thought, encouraging me to read more of his oeuvre. I followed up on that idea with <em>The Wanting Seed</em>, which he wrote immediately following Clockwork. This dystopian novel demonstrates one of his persistent themes, the conflict between 'Augustinian' authoritarianism and 'neo-Pelagian' liberalism. The novel is set in a future similar to <em>A Clockwork Orange</em>, where Burgess projects an England in which Christianity, fertility, and heterosexuality will have been outlawed. His heroine, Beatrice-Joanna, is a dissident earth-mother who runs away to Wales to give birth in the home of her brother-in-law. Her husband, Tristram, is a history teacher who, in an early scene in the novel, explains the history and meaning of pelphase (Pelagianism) and gusphase (Augustinianism), while his brother heads the Ministry of Infertility. The brothers' relationship leads Tristram to think, “If you expect the worst from a person you can never be disappointed.”  Using an almost over-the-top comic style Burgess comments on themes including: the tyranny of the state, homosexuality, perpetual war, spontaneous orgies, the persistence of religious feeling, and cannibalism. After his escape from prison Tristram hitches a ride from a sort of local militia-man who comments:  "There doesn't seem to be a government at the moment, but we're trying to improvise some kind of regional law and order. . . We can't have all this, indiscriminate cannibalism and the drains out of order.  We've got our wives and children to think of." (pp 171-2)  Although the setting of the novel demonstrates the worst aspects of pelagian liberalism and addresses many societal issues, the primary subject is overpopulation and its relation to culture.<br /><br />The novel is inventive with a comic seriousness that is humorous with periodic moments of unease; the line between the comic and the serious is sometimes blurred. The author's signature fecundity of ideas, his love of quotations and literary allusions, and his brilliant use of language carries the reader through the rough spots. However, it is not hard to understand why it was "considered too daring" by potential backers of Carlo Ponti's proposed film version. My admiration for Burgess as a novelist of ideas grows with each of his novels. This comically heretical entry, combines with its predecessor to provide a veritable one-two punch of dystopian delight.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16392823078160519527" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText3411228874429169685" style="display:none">Burgess has created a dystopia in which it's a sin to have sisters, a crime to have children, and a large ancestry can cost you your job. <div id="review_2861997518" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2861997518" />
    <a title="tortoise dreams" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51546630-tortoise-dreams"><img alt="tortoise dreams" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456768521p2/51546630.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2861997518?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="tortoise dreams" class="user" itemprop="url" name="tortoise dreams" href="/user/show/51546630-tortoise-dreams">tortoise dreams</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2861997518" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8306100912223364237">Upon (short) reflection, this story is more complex than it seemed on reading. The two main themes are a malthusian nightmare that leads to food shortages & odd societal responses, & the the strands of history that guide the apparently unguidable. Must note that <i>The Wanting Seed</i> is not for those who insist on political correctness in their books, who can't deal with the rough & tumble of the "real" world. This is not for those who insist that a writer from the early Sixties write as if he was wr</span>
  <span id="freeText8306100912223364237" style="display:none">Upon (short) reflection, this story is more complex than it seemed on reading. The two main themes are a malthusian nightmare that leads to food shortages &amp; odd societal responses, &amp; the the strands of history that guide the apparently unguidable. Must note that <i>The Wanting Seed</i> is not for those who insist on political correctness in their books, who can't deal with the rough &amp; tumble of the "real" world. This is not for those who insist that a writer from the early Sixties write as if he was writing in 2019. At that time ZPG &amp; overpopulation must've been overwhelming concerns -- something we hear much less than the current fears of climate change. For me it was interesting that some of the effects that Burgess predicted due to overpopulation (e.g., multiculturalism &amp; the social acceptance &amp; perceived advantages of homosexuality), have occurred, but not as threats to societal stability. Published the same year as his more renowned <i>A Clockwork Orange</i>, this is also a dystopian (is anyone getting tired of that word?) journey. Despite his immense vocabulary, this was an easy though not quick read, mostly linear &amp; told with a light &amp; comic tone until the last couple pages. During the read I was right there moving along, but looking back Burgess hits so many issues from religion &amp; cannibalism (they overlap) to the fragility &amp; resilience of family &amp; society. Not quite the masterwork that is <i>A Clockwork Orange</i>, but a worthy companion. While reading it often reminded me of <i>Brave New World</i>, &amp; while I don't think it's quite to the level of that one or the other early classics such as <i>1984</i>, <i>Fahrenheit 451</i>, or <i>The Handmaid's Tale</i>, it certainly deserves a seat at the table &amp; a place in the conversation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8306100912223364237" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2861997518">

<div id="review_751181653" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/751181653" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1564654-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1222373756p2/1564654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/751181653?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/1564654-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1564654-mark?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1564654-mark?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer751181653&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer751181653" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3387625100244822113">Situationally, <i>The Wanting Seed</i> reminded me of one of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4764.Philip_K__Dick' title='Philip K. Dick' rel='nofollow noopener'>Philip K. Dick</a>'s more goofball novels (without the goofball inventiveness of PKD's futuristic concepts). In the future, a profoundly overpopulated earth contains a placid, "liberal" (in this case, as defined by the Catholic Burgess, meaning Godless and permissive) society where homosexuality, medically induced infertility, and childlessness are encouraged (but not strictly enforced, this being a "liberal" society and all). <br/><br/>Tristram Foxe, a hi</span>
  <span id="freeText3387625100244822113" style="display:none">Situationally, <i>The Wanting Seed</i> reminded me of one of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4764.Philip_K__Dick" title="Philip K. Dick" rel="nofollow noopener">Philip K. Dick</a>'s more goofball novels (without the goofball inventiveness of PKD's futuristic concepts). In the future, a profoundly overpopulated earth contains a placid, "liberal" (in this case, as defined by the Catholic Burgess, meaning Godless and permissive) society where homosexuality, medically induced infertility, and childlessness are encouraged (but not strictly enforced, this being a "liberal" society and all). <br /><br />Tristram Foxe, a history teacher, and his wife, Beatrice-Joanna, have just lost their infant son. Beatrice-Joanna is also having an affair with Tristram's brother Derek, a government official who pretends to be gay in order to climb the ladder of success. But the overpopulation, combined with a worldwide blight, causes unrest, which in turn causes the government to begin enforcing its suggestions as laws. Just when Tristram finds out about his wife's and brother's affair, he gets caught up in a food-shortage riot outside his building and is arrested. While he is in prison, the government completes its shift from what Burgess calls the Pelagian Phase to an Intermediate Phase (on its way, eventually, to the Augustinian Phase), wherein the previously liberal government becomes a repressive dictatorship. The once-placid world gives way to police roundups, rampant cannibalism, a resurgence of Christianity, and war.<br /><br />Billed as a satire, I found <i>The Wanting Seed</i> to be plenty absurd, but not particularly funny. Fifty years ago (the book was written in 1962), I have no doubt that much of the "humor" was intended to be derived from the unnatural homosexual behavior of a few of the principal characters and several of the incidental characters. (I don't put "unnatural" in scare quotes above because the idea here is that the homosexual behavior of these characters is truly unnatural. That is, they would be heterosexual if it were up to them, but because of society's strictures, they are essentially forced to ACT gay in that stereotypical, homophobic way, with lots of mincing around, simpering, etc.--in addition to having sex with members of the same sex. Of course, in Burgess's view, clearly, <i>all</i> homosexuality is unnatural, thus making the use of the word "unnatural" redundant in this context, as far as he's concerned.) <br /><br />Setting aside the outdated view that one's sexual orientation is a choice (i.e., that gay people are gay because they decided to be, not because it's a part of their genetic makeup)--not to mention to absurdity of believing that any society could successfully repress an entire civilization's sexual orientation (good luck convincing a horny teenage boy that he can't have sex with girls [or with boys, if that's his preference] because it's good for society)--why must the new norm of homosexuality in the distant future require the perceived characteristics of gay men circa 1962? Did the ancient Spartans sashay around with limp wrists, saying things like "Oh my gawd, bitch, where did you get those shoes"? Unlikely. Burgess was a Catholic, so his limited vision is not entirely surprising, but it still reveals his serious misunderstanding of how sexual attraction to one's own gender works, not to mention his inability to conceive of a new way in which men of the future, who have sex with other men, might act. In the end, Burgess used homosexuality as nothing more than a useful shorthand for showing how a Godless society has ruined itself.<br /><br />As such, <i>The Wanting Seed</i> is embarrassingly dated, not to mention fairly unbelievable. And this unbelievability extends to other parts of the novel as well. When the civilization devolves, quite quickly, from a sterile, basically vegetarian society to a cannibalistic one, no one (including the main character) seems to have an even initial reaction of disgust to breaking one of the fundamental taboos of Western culture. I am reminded of the sci-fi novel <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/218467.Lucifer_s_Hammer" title="Lucifer's Hammer by Larry Niven" rel="nofollow noopener">Lucifer's Hammer</a>
</i> in which a band of survivors after a worldwide catastrophe engage in cannibalism as a way of acclimating themselves to it, in case it becomes an absolute necessity as the world crumbles around them. But no one's happy about it, and most of the members of the group are pretty much forced to partake, because the thought of doing it voluntarily is, at first, too repellant. Not so in <i>The Wanting Seed</i>, where everyone seems pretty much OK with it (even enthusiastic about it) from the start. <br /><br />Speaking of cannibalism, I found it equally unlikely that Tristram, after having escaped prison and trying to track down his wife during a brief period of lawlessness, is taken in by a small town of cannibals and treated generously. When he first arrived, I naturally believed that they would try to lure him in with their smiles and then eat him. But no, they were happy to feed him and send him on his way, which leads me to wonder where, exactly, they're getting all this human meat they're eating. Would they rather eat their own friends, family, and neighbors than some drifter passing through? I'm no cannibal, so maybe I just don't get it, but that seems unlikely to me.<br /><br />The one part I found entirely believable is the final section, in which the unsuspecting and gullible are conscripted into a newly organized army (as wars had been a thing of the distant past) and forced to fight one's own countrymen, unknowingly, as a way of generating vast amounts of human meat for the remaining population. A sinister concept that rings true in an era where wars are already waged for ignominious reasons. For this (and for Burgess's usual lexical dexterity) I give the book two stars instead of one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3387625100244822113" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mel" href="/user/show/5866692-mel">Mel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5866692-mel?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5866692-mel?shelf=20th-century-fiction">20th-century-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer188775165" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14932004838958993520">I have to say the original "dystopia" sounded totally Utopian to me. In the world, homosexuality was encouraged, breeding was discouraged, race was something that was ignored and everyone had enough to eat, there was no war, no military, no religion, there was a liberal government that seemed half way between communism and anarchy. Of course the homophobic main characters didn't get a lot of sympathy from me, but I loved the setting. Of course things didn't stay that way as society was changing,</span>
  <span id="freeText14932004838958993520" style="display:none">I have to say the original "dystopia" sounded totally Utopian to me. In the world, homosexuality was encouraged, breeding was discouraged, race was something that was ignored and everyone had enough to eat, there was no war, no military, no religion, there was a liberal government that seemed half way between communism and anarchy. Of course the homophobic main characters didn't get a lot of sympathy from me, but I loved the setting. Of course things didn't stay that way as society was changing, the state became totalitarian, filled with secret police and torture, which led to LOTS of cannibalism, paganism, sex, and the creation of an army. The characters in the book were a little underwhelming. Though I did like the fact that the main character was opposed to all the different governments. But really this book was about the setting, different reactions to a chronically overpopulated planet. It was disturbing and great. I shall definitely read more Burgess.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14932004838958993520" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1001865416" />
    <a title="Jacob Anderson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6044813-jacob-anderson"><img alt="Jacob Anderson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1377834653p2/6044813.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1001865416?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacob Anderson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacob Anderson" href="/user/show/6044813-jacob-anderson">Jacob Anderson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1001865416" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5817861661443274112">It's a good thing I chose to read A Clockwork Orange (my favorite novel) first. Had The Wanting Seed been my introduction to Burgess's work, I'd never feel inclined to approach any of his writing again. It's boring, unfocused, silly, unrealistic, cliched, homophobic, racist and misogynistic. In regard to those latter few complaints, it's true that an author is a product of his or her time period. Great literature may contain offensive themes. This is not great literature, though. The characters </span>
  <span id="freeText5817861661443274112" style="display:none">It's a good thing I chose to read A Clockwork Orange (my favorite novel) first. Had The Wanting Seed been my introduction to Burgess's work, I'd never feel inclined to approach any of his writing again. It's boring, unfocused, silly, unrealistic, cliched, homophobic, racist and misogynistic. In regard to those latter few complaints, it's true that an author is a product of his or her time period. Great literature may contain offensive themes. This is not great literature, though. The characters act solely out of self-concern; it's near impossible to care for any of them. The dystopian elements aren't well-developed either. Burgess clearly doesn't create a realistic futuristic situation, but it's not some sort of believable imperfect alternate universe either. Also, the writing is so pretentious. Especially towards the end, I found myself skimming more than reading. I'd lost all interest, and solely wanted to be able to know that I'd at least finished the book.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2368269944" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2368269944" />
    <a title="Victoria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4093195-victoria"><img alt="Victoria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1465667678p2/4093195.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2368269944?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Victoria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Victoria" href="/user/show/4093195-victoria">Victoria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9785170940721" href="/book/show/34439718">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4093195-victoria?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2368269944" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5810098441770999750">Cannibalism. Sexism. Homosexuality. Totalitarian regime. Religion. How is all this connected, you may ask? All these notions are combined in this novel by the famous author of <i>A Clockwork Orange</i>.<br/><br/><i>The Wanting Seed</i> (1962) is a dystopian novel about an overpopulated world where a special organization is controlling child birth and does nothing to ensure that newborns stay alive. The story is told from two points of view: <br/>- Tristram - a plodding but conscientious history teacher<br/>- Beatrice-Joanna - hi</span>
  <span id="freeText5810098441770999750" style="display:none">Cannibalism. Sexism. Homosexuality. Totalitarian regime. Religion. How is all this connected, you may ask? All these notions are combined in this novel by the famous author of <i>A Clockwork Orange</i>.<br /><br /><i>The Wanting Seed</i> (1962) is a dystopian novel about an overpopulated world where a special organization is controlling child birth and does nothing to ensure that newborns stay alive. The story is told from two points of view: <br />- Tristram - a plodding but conscientious history teacher<br />- Beatrice-Joanna - his beautiful, but unfaithful wife<br />Their only child had died and having more kids is forbidden by law.<br /><br />As in most dystopian stories, by the middle of the book chaos rules the world, most notions are mixed up and the characters eagerly try to find their place in this new system. <br /><br />The first half of the book is really exciting, intriguing and thought-provoking. However, the second part is rather slow-paced and very confusing, as if the author was trying to fit in all these ideas and action scenes in the few pages he had left, forgetting that there's someone on the other side reading it all. The ending was quite shocking and disappointing. Don't want to spoil it for others, but if you want to discuss the ending, leave your comments below my review or send me a private message. I'm really interested in what others have to say about it. <br /><br />Nonetheless, this book is worth reading. However, I was impressed by <i>Kallocain</i> (another dystopian novel by a Swedish writer) much more. This book contains many disturbing scenes, so if you're not into reading about issues I mentioned in the beginning, then this book is not for you.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5810098441770999750" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <span id="freeText13155582369229259946" style="display:none">I read this about a week or two ago, but it's already fading in my memory.<br /><br />I guess the basic premise is that the world is overpopulated, so you're limited to how many children you can have. But polite, genteel people don't have any.<br /><br />Which has a knockoff effect of, if you're gay (particularly male and gay) you advance more quickly in your career, and things like that. So there's a real advantage to pretending to be gay. And the culture has adopted gay dress and mannerisms. Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy is silly, but.. you can feel for Arthur Dent and Ford Prefect is fun, and some of the other characters are fun and/or interesting. And in this book, just.. no, you can't really like them. <span id="freeTextContainer12858689665939940377">If you like unbelievable use of Vocabulary you will like Anthony Burgess. And Burgess' novel The Wanting Seed will not let you down in that fashion. Overall the novel is dealing with population control in a futuristic dystopian society. More acute themes deal with desires, sexual, for life, and love. And how one can never give up trying to pursue what they believe in... especially love. The wanting seed is pretty epic as it follows the life of the main character Tristram. The journey reminded me</span>
  <span id="freeText12858689665939940377" style="display:none">If you like unbelievable use of Vocabulary you will like Anthony Burgess. And Burgess' novel The Wanting Seed will not let you down in that fashion. Overall the novel is dealing with population control in a futuristic dystopian society. More acute themes deal with desires, sexual, for life, and love. And how one can never give up trying to pursue what they believe in... especially love. The wanting seed is pretty epic as it follows the life of the main character Tristram. The journey reminded me a lot of Peer Gynt's journey in Ibsen's play. We get to watch the main character grow up and change over a wild adventure that leads him all over Britain. The beginning and end of the book are wonderful. The storytelling is very crisp and enticing. However, the middle of the book gets muddled down, and seems to lose focus. Thus, the three stars. <br />However, if you like linguistics and vocabulary, or other novels from Anthony Burgess, this is a must read.</span>

<div id="review_491109918" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/491109918" />
    <a title="Angela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6473570-angela"><img alt="Angela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362368934p2/6473570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/491109918?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angela" href="/user/show/6473570-angela">Angela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer491109918" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14447394471124741551">For a while there, I really wasn't sure where this book was going, or why it was worth my time to read it. But, I stayed the course, and I have to admit, I had a hard time putting it down in the final few chapters as the book reached its climax and attempted resolution. The final chapter was pure elegance, and while I don't don't feel it resolved the plot well enough for my taste, I appreciated the power of Burgess' prose. <br/><br/>The problem with reading this book today is quarter-fold. First of all, B</span>
  <span id="freeText14447394471124741551" style="display:none">For a while there, I really wasn't sure where this book was going, or why it was worth my time to read it. But, I stayed the course, and I have to admit, I had a hard time putting it down in the final few chapters as the book reached its climax and attempted resolution. The final chapter was pure elegance, and while I don't don't feel it resolved the plot well enough for my taste, I appreciated the power of Burgess' prose. <br /><br />The problem with reading this book today is quarter-fold. First of all, Burgess was British, so his book is heavy in English jargon and colloquialisms, many of which were unfamiliar to me...and would be, I imagine, to most Americans. Secondly, Burgess wrote this book 50 years ago, which further distances his language from a contemporary audience. Thirdly, he is writing of a future time in the mode of science fiction, which introduces more unfamiliar concepts, words, ideas to his audience. I had a hard time distinguishing the science fiction language from the Britishisms and archaic language. And finally, Burgess is writing satire, so much like Swift's "A Modest Proposal," it relys very heavily on one's understanding of the nuances of social/politial issues of the time in which it was written.<br /><br />This is a very dense book. The subject matter is difficult (society's relation to God, homosexuality, cannabalism, etc.) and the context is highly philosophical and allusory, which makes for really deep themes, if one has the background knowledge to assist in extracting all the subtext. Go for it if you want a challenge, but I warn you: caveat emptor.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14447394471124741551" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_236416301" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/236416301" />
    <a title="Shayna L" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/337084-shayna-l"><img alt="Shayna L" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1188773710p2/337084.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/236416301?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shayna L" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shayna L" href="/user/show/337084-shayna-l">Shayna L</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer236416301" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7851686094947117800">This is very much a future dystopia novel. It takes place in what was once the UK and follows the lives of a married couple, Tristram and Beatrice-Joanna. They are heterosexual couple living in a world where homosexuality and castration are heavily encouraged in order to curb the out of control world population. Breeding is shunned, a social faux pas. As poor crops and government law begin to come down on the people, they change to answer in strange and frightening ways.<br/>It's an interesting take </span>
  <span id="freeText7851686094947117800" style="display:none">This is very much a future dystopia novel. It takes place in what was once the UK and follows the lives of a married couple, Tristram and Beatrice-Joanna. They are heterosexual couple living in a world where homosexuality and castration are heavily encouraged in order to curb the out of control world population. Breeding is shunned, a social faux pas. As poor crops and government law begin to come down on the people, they change to answer in strange and frightening ways.<br />It's an interesting take on human response to massive overpopulation. There's a lot of really good commentary on the way people as a whole might respond to global emergency. The story itself isn't difficult to follow, but it moves through phases rather quickly once it gets going and the state of the government seems to change very rapidly. Burgess also uses quite the vocabulary. There were a number of obscure words that not even my dictionary could help me with. Made me feel dumb real fast.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7851686094947117800" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/454803857" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14534950-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505346791p2/14534950.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/14534950-david">David</a>

  <span id="freeText9233341146324761559" style="display:none">mind you - &amp; at once this sounds like the opening for Tin Drum - but i have just finished the book &amp; it's fresh in my mind. there's been a slew of negative reviews ~ right here on Goodreads ~ saying this is dated &amp; non-PC, &amp; apparently not seen for the sci-fi satire it is. <br /><br />2) II 'Mind You;' as i have a 99 IQ, Asperger's &amp; may not have 'gotten' that it is *indeed* dated &amp; hateful, but welcome to All Things British - even old Ireland isn't spared! but, yes: read: browse, if you enjoy brief, what-if satire, references to Joyce &amp; Rabelais, &amp; witty, cutting bits of dialog tween all-TOO-British types *destined* to live forever in the past -- though, by Burgess' telling, in an ever tidepool cycle of Pelagianism/Augustinian, Whig-Tory 'anglo saxon attitudes.' <br /><br />in sum: FUN (if you're not a pedantic prude)</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer154483624" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer298525594521373970">Mildly offensive (homophobic) with an air of hysteria. The lament for traditional gender roles leaves me particularly cold.<br/><br/>The style is visual and emotional and enriches the text to the extent that the author's failure to anticipate technological advances (a general feature of quality dystopian fiction) in, for example, controlling reproduction and surveillance, is mostly unnoticed.<br/><br/>The novel is clearly demarcated into three parts (the urban, rural and military experiences of the protagonist) whi</span>
  <span id="freeText298525594521373970" style="display:none">Mildly offensive (homophobic) with an air of hysteria. The lament for traditional gender roles leaves me particularly cold.<br /><br />The style is visual and emotional and enriches the text to the extent that the author's failure to anticipate technological advances (a general feature of quality dystopian fiction) in, for example, controlling reproduction and surveillance, is mostly unnoticed.<br /><br />The novel is clearly demarcated into three parts (the urban, rural and military experiences of the protagonist) which mirror the three phases that the society is supposed to turn through, giving the whole novel a kind of satisfying symmetry.<br /><br />The gay paramilitaries beating up honest heteros and persecuting breeders? Puh-leez.</span>
  <a data-text-id="298525594521373970" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('VdNIiVvMF9b82S5HusptYyLrRgVphKLAeu0g+pWaGYGXA1BDUITwJeeIfsPoWw7rx5aaPMgbrcLNSWfj0hmr0g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Ed4LOmMjoKaOUkdEruzJLE1Sy23azRoHXeOfWAFSXeLTDhPwaGtHVZUDF8D8faqkqC8XVHtSFQXqR9hBRtHvsQ==')})
  }

To the reader of 1922 it presented a shocking look at a little known phenomenon. Today, while we are more familiar with drugs because of their widespread use in our culture, Diary of a Drug Fiend remains one o fthe most intense, detailed and accurate accounts of drug addiction and the drug experience.\n\nThe book was written by Crowley after years of deep personal study and experimentation with drugs. It is the story of a young man and woman who fall madly in love and whirl through Europe in a frenzied haze of heroin/cocaine adventure. Their ecstacy is brought to an abrupt end when their drug supply is cut off and despair replaces joy. Through the guidance of King Lamus, a master Adept, they free themselves from the entanglements of addition by the application of practical Magick.\n\nThe narrative carries the reader aloft through the brilliance of the imagery created by this master of language; his prose development parallels the growth and increasing depth of his characters in an uncanny fashion. This is a book to be read and reread. It will also prove a useful document to doctors, lawyers, police and addicts for its unique and precise presentation of the psychology of addiction and the possibility of its cure through the <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11980126351465574228"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name.</i><br/><br/>Anthony Burgess was a British novelist, critic and composer. He was also a librettist, poet, playwright, screenwriter, essayist, travel writer, broadcaster, translator, linguist and educationalist. Born in Manchester, he lived for long periods in Southeast Asia, the USA and Mediterranean Europe as well as in Eng</span>
  <span id="freeText11980126351465574228" style="display:none"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name.</i><br /><br />Anthony Burgess was a British novelist, critic and composer. He was also a librettist, poet, playwright, screenwriter, essayist, travel writer, broadcaster, translator, linguist and educationalist. Born in Manchester, he lived for long periods in Southeast Asia, the USA and Mediterranean Europe as well as in England. His fiction includes the Malayan trilogy (The Long Day Wanes) on the dying days of Britain's empire in the East; the Enderby quartet of novels about a poet and his muse; Nothing Like the Sun, a recreation of Shakespeare's love-life; A Clockwork Orange, an exploration of the nature of evil; and Earthly Powers, a panoramic saga of the 20th century. He published studies of Joyce, Hemingway, Shakespeare and Lawrence, produced the treatises on linguistics Language Made Plain and A Mouthful of Air, and was a prolific journalist, writing in several languages. He translated and adapted Cyrano de Bergerac, Oedipus the King, and Carmen for the stage; scripted Jesus of Nazareth and Moses the Lawgiver for the screen; invented the prehistoric language spoken in Quest for Fire; and composed the Sinfoni Melayu, the Symphony (No. 3) in C, and the opera Blooms of Dublin.<br />-Wikipedia<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11980126351465574228" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
